<div id="review_2850475304" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2850475304" />
    <a title="Meike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59050228-meike"><img alt="Meike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514995700p2/59050228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2850475304?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meike" href="/user/show/59050228-meike">Meike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780241384503" href="/book/show/41715730-if-beale-street-could-talk">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59050228-meike?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59050228-meike?shelf=2019-read">2019-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2850475304" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15730108928878549140">This is a brilliant novel about systemic racism and its social and psychological effects, but it is also a book about the strength of black families and communities as well as the power of solidarity. Set in Harlem in the 1970's, Baldwin tells the story of Tish and Fonny, two young lovers who have known each other all their lives. Fonny gets falsely accused of a crime he didn't commit and is wrongfully imprisoned, then Tish finds out she's pregnant - what will become of their romantic love story</span>
  <span id="freeText15730108928878549140" style="display:none">This is a brilliant novel about systemic racism and its social and psychological effects, but it is also a book about the strength of black families and communities as well as the power of solidarity. Set in Harlem in the 1970's, Baldwin tells the story of Tish and Fonny, two young lovers who have known each other all their lives. Fonny gets falsely accused of a crime he didn't commit and is wrongfully imprisoned, then Tish finds out she's pregnant - what will become of their romantic love story now?<br /><br />As the novel progresses, we get to meet a whole cast of characters who make up the families and social circle of Trish and Fonny, and we get to hear about their struggles with racism as well as their strategies to stick together in the face of an injust system that is created to hold them down. In some ways, Tayari Jones' much-lauded <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33590210.An_American_Marriage" title="An American Marriage by Tayari Jones" rel="nofollow noopener">An American Marriage</a> seems to reflect Baldwin's novel, but transports it into the future: While at first seems like the lovers can now participate in the American Dream, Jones goes on to show that racism has persisted (the story takes a turn when the husband is wrongfully imprisoned for the exact same crime as Fonny, and Jones also discusses the repercussions for his whole family and social circle) - but Baldwin is the far, far superior writer.<br /><br />When Baldwin wrote "Beale Street", he had already left the States and was living in France. The story was inspired by his friend Tony Maynard who (in the US) was sent to jail for six years although he was innocent. While Baldwin was firmly convinced that the American prison system served the goal to suppress black people (which is still happening, just check out <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35167682.A_Colony_in_a_Nation" title="A Colony in a Nation by Christopher L. Hayes" rel="nofollow noopener">A Colony in a Nation</a>), he also believed in the power of love, and "Beale Street" reflects that in intense ways.<br /><br />The book's title was taken from W. C. Handy's <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=MKnAub5-LNE" rel="nofollow noopener">Beale Street Blues</a>, so if you want to know what it means, just listen to the song! :-) I haven't seen the "Beale Street" movie yet, but the trailer looks amazing: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=N4m3t3G3Zqc">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=N4m3t...</a><br /><br />Like all of Baldwin's writing, this book is highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15730108928878549140" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2617338346" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2617338346" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2617338346?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2617338346" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5635493939695958870">The vulnerability of black bodies, is what James Baldwin is talking about, once again showing why he's the spiritual progenitor of Ta-Nehisi Coates. Fonny looks the wrong way at the wrong cop, and the next thing you know his body is in jail for rape. He's innocent, but what does that have to do with anything?<br/><br/>You know how in a romantic comedy, you have these characters that ought to be together but some sort of dramatic contrivance or misunderstanding is introduced to keep them apart?<br/><br/><b>Top seven re</b></span>
  <span id="freeText5635493939695958870" style="display:none">The vulnerability of black bodies, is what James Baldwin is talking about, once again showing why he's the spiritual progenitor of Ta-Nehisi Coates. Fonny looks the wrong way at the wrong cop, and the next thing you know his body is in jail for rape. He's innocent, but what does that have to do with anything?<br /><br />You know how in a romantic comedy, you have these characters that ought to be together but some sort of dramatic contrivance or misunderstanding is introduced to keep them apart?<br /><br /><b>Top seven reasons stars get crossed</b><br />7. Wrong sides of tracks<br />6. She dead<br />5. Siblings<br />4. Are married to dudes who are bffs and suspect nothing<br />3. He's a drummer who vapes<br />2. She's a teenager and he's like 50<br />1. A racist system that incarcerates him for looking at the wrong cop on the wrong day.<br /><br />And just like that, Fonny and Tish, at the very first step of their lives together, are torn apart.<br /><br />There's no better writer in the 20th century than James Baldwin, so even his minor work is incredibly good, but this is a minor work. It's didactic, is the major problem: there's a lot of Furious Stylesish exposition. A third act trip to Puerto Rico doesn't fit that well, either; it's a good story but it makes the book flow weird. And there are a few...period touches that are jarring. History hasn't aged gracefully. Tish does a lot of drinking while pregnant, is what I'm saying. The scene where the two families meet to announce that they're now bound through Tish's pregnancy, and what are they going to do about it...Fonny's mother and sisters are sortof fun in their outlandish cartoon villain way, but a touch of domestic violence doesn't make the point it thinks it's making. <br /><br />We've finally caught up to what Baldwin was pointing out in 1974 - that the mass incarceration of black men amounts to a new Jim Crow, an entire system still devoted to destroying black families - and here comes the movie version of this book, out right now by the Moonlight guy. It's probably super good and I bet he leaves that domestic violence scene right out. Maybe he'll change the method of starcrossing, too. Like, maybe it's just, his bookstore buys her bookstore or whatever. This plot is kindof a bummer.<br /><br />I started a playlist but it fizzled out, but <a href="https://open.spotify.com/user/alexrehmss/playlist/5F1xdN6slkflAxQ8ZNZ22N?si=8e0wfjIhQA6EwJR996-2Zw" title="Spotify link" rel="nofollow noopener">here it is.</a> It's got the song that inspired the title, a song mentioned in the book, and then Meek Mill's brilliant "Intro," recorded just after he got out of jail for some bullshit. The idea was to put a bunch more rap songs by African-American people who've been thrown in jail for bullshit, but I couldn't think of any off the top of my head and whatever. No, you do not need to suggest Black Steel in the Hour of Chaos, it doesn't technically fit my theme.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5635493939695958870" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2900733055" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2900733055" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1788328684796529592"><b> I guess it can’t be to often that two people can laugh and make love, too, make love because they are laughing, laugh because they’re making love. The love and the laughter come from the same place: but not many people go there. </b><br/><br/>This tale should be the sweetest love story ever told, and in many ways indeed Tish and Fonny are as true to each other and as much in love as Romeo and his Juliet. But the world they live in is not a fairytale or a stage, but a place where simply having the wrong kind </span>
  <span id="freeText1788328684796529592" style="display:none"><b> I guess it can’t be to often that two people can laugh and make love, too, make love because they are laughing, laugh because they’re making love. The love and the laughter come from the same place: but not many people go there. </b><br /><br />This tale should be the sweetest love story ever told, and in many ways indeed Tish and Fonny are as true to each other and as much in love as Romeo and his Juliet. But the world they live in is not a fairytale or a stage, but a place where simply having the wrong kind of skin or the ‘nappy’ hair can land you in jail.<br /><br /><b> They looked at us as though we were zebras – and, you know, some people like zebras and some people don’t. But nobody ever asks the zebras. </b><br /><br />When I heard about the movie, before finding out there’s a novel too, I thought this would be a musical about Harlem Renaissance. Music plays a big part in the story still (Ray Charles and Gospels in the Church, and Jazz from Tish’s mother) but what James Baldwin does here is to put down a poem in prose for those people who don’t matter in the eyes of society because they were born poor and ‘zebras’. Baldwin makes Beale Street talk as only Toni Morrison and James Sallis have done for me in the past. [No offense to Angie Thomas, whose novel I read and loved earlier this year, but when it comes to style, Baldwin is intense, moving, authentic, painful while in the other case it was the story that impressed me despite the lackluster YA prose]<br /><br /><b> Sometimes, I admit, I’m scared – because nobody can take the shit they throw on us forever. But then, you just have to somehow fix your mind to get from one day to the next. If you think too far ahead, if you even ‘try’ to think too far ahead, you’ll never make it. </b> <br /><br />There is little I could write down here about a plot : boy and girl grow up together, they fall in love, society pulls them apart, they can only rely on each other and on their families to make it through the dark times.<br /><br /><b> Listen, little girl. We got to take care of each other in this world, right? </b><br /><br />I hope the next Tish and Fonny to fall in love will have an easier time than the ones described in this novel. All signs point in the opposite direction for now, but their lives matter to me, as they should matter to all of us, regardless of colour or creed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1788328684796529592" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3034031874" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3034031874" />
    <a title="Daniel Chaikin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18695911-daniel-chaikin"><img alt="Daniel Chaikin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534027159p2/18695911.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3034031874?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Chaikin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Chaikin" href="/user/show/18695911-daniel-chaikin">Daniel Chaikin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3034031874" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4831193295784014195">The essay collection <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/256105.No_Name_in_the_Street' title='No Name in the Street by James Baldwin' rel='nofollow noopener'>No Name in the Street</a> includes the story of a friend of Baldwin's who was charged with a murder he couldn't have committed and spent over ten years in prison, despite the high-paid lawyer Baldwin found for him. That experience is the inspiration for this fictional look at the criminal justice system in New York through Tish, a young woman from Harlem who got pregnant just before her fiance, Fonny, got locked up as a suspect in a black-on-white rape. <br/><br/>Here Baldwin tones down h</span>
  <span id="freeText4831193295784014195" style="display:none">The essay collection <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/256105.No_Name_in_the_Street" title="No Name in the Street by James Baldwin" rel="nofollow noopener">No Name in the Street</a> includes the story of a friend of Baldwin's who was charged with a murder he couldn't have committed and spent over ten years in prison, despite the high-paid lawyer Baldwin found for him. That experience is the inspiration for this fictional look at the criminal justice system in New York through Tish, a young woman from Harlem who got pregnant just before her fiance, Fonny, got locked up as a suspect in a black-on-white rape. <br /><br />Here Baldwin tones down his writing. There are a lot of sort of pyrotechnics I have associated with Baldwin that are simply stripped out of this story. There is no showmanship, or performance, or hip society, or counter-culture relationship, or anything shocking to the reader or societal norms. That's new. Tish is just a nice simple kid from a nice family with issues, and Fonny is the same - and Baldwin, who loves issues, piles them on. The sexist Harlem culture almost seems to get a blessing. But in here with Tish and Fonny is a straight-forward love story, except that it takes place in a strange world where police are really dangerous and arbitrary. Fonny will get pinned for a rape he couldn't have committed by a bad cop, and as the system evolves, where blacks are basically guilty-till-proven-innocent, he ends up in prison a long time waiting, hoping, dealing, pondering options. <br /><br />This sort of a humbled Baldwin creates a structure piece, by which I mean that the way the story is presented adds up and manages, in the end, to powerfully convey its message - the stress and suffering of prison experience, not only for the innocently incarcerated losing his youth, but for his family and his lover and soulmate separated for some indeterminate and desperate long time.<br /><br />-----------------------------------------------<br /><br />55. <b>If Beale Street Could Talk by James Baldwin</b><br /><b>published</b>: 1974<br /><b>format</b>: 197 page paperback, 2006 edition, 25th printing<br /><b>acquired</b>: July <br /><b>read</b>: Nov 1, 11-13<br /><b>time reading</b>: 5 hr 0 min, 1.5 min/page<br /><b>rating</b>: 4</span>
  <a data-text-id="4831193295784014195" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2646281279" />
    <a title="Katharine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/373950-katharine"><img alt="Katharine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589499749p2/373950.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2646281279?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katharine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katharine" href="/user/show/373950-katharine">Katharine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2646281279" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11016530384135624361">This was my first book of Baldwin's. Knowing I wanted to see the movie, I figured reading this beforehand would be a good place to start.<br/><br/>I want to preface this by saying that writing this is difficult for me. As a white woman writing a review of a book written by an AOC about POC, I want to acknowledge that my perspective is different and there might be things I missed. In fact, I do feel like I missed something because I wasn't as emotionally invested in Tish and Fonnie's relationship as I thou</span>
  <span id="freeText11016530384135624361" style="display:none">This was my first book of Baldwin's. Knowing I wanted to see the movie, I figured reading this beforehand would be a good place to start.<br /><br />I want to preface this by saying that writing this is difficult for me. As a white woman writing a review of a book written by an AOC about POC, I want to acknowledge that my perspective is different and there might be things I missed. In fact, I do feel like I missed something because I wasn't as emotionally invested in Tish and Fonnie's relationship as I thought I would to be.<br /><br />One of the things that impressed me the most was that a man was able to write from a woman's point of view and do it so incredibly well. I also loved Tish's family -- they came alive on the page for me, probably more than anything else in the book. I loved how fiercely they all supported Tish, how dedicated they were to getting Fonnie out of prison, and how they stood up for themselves against Fonnie's family (particularly his mothers and sisters).<br /><br />Maybe the length is part of what made it hard for me to feel connected to the story -- at just under 200 pages it's a pretty quick read. However, Baldwin does a great job of packing in a lot of different emotions, the biggest one I felt while reading was tension -- between Tish and Fonnie's families, in Tish herself as she grapples with what might happen, and with Fonnie as he wrestles with the injustice of being wrongly imprisoned.<br /><br />I'll admit that before writing this review (but after reading the book) I saw the movie, and I felt much more connected to the characters (especially Fonnie) after seeing it. I won't say much more because I don't want to spoil it for anyone who hasn't yet seen it, but I highly recommend reading the book and then following it up with the movie.<br /><br />Overall I found Baldwin's writing to be beautiful and was moved by the injustice of what happened to them. I also appreciated this portrayal of black romance (in some ways it was reminiscent of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33590210.An_American_Marriage" title="An American Marriage by Tayari Jones" rel="nofollow noopener">An American Marriage</a> -- or I should say that in hindsight, An American Marriage felt reminiscent of this), something that has been lacking in my reading life (and something that I will be intentionally seeking out more of). One of the biggest takeaways for me was just how little has changed in the 40+ years since this story was written and just how far we have to go for black people to truly be treated as equal in this country.<br /><br />Please make sure to read these #ownvoices reviews: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1821445244?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">one by RodKelly</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2591451433?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">one by Jessie </a></span>
  <a data-text-id="11016530384135624361" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2490686617" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/6041973-josh">Josh</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2490686617" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer5110243256204076679">When it comes to writing a review for this, I prefer to share a small afterword as I prefer to let Baldwin's words speak rather than mine.<br/><br/><i>"I sat on the hassock, leaning on Daddy's knee. Now, it was seven o'clock and the streets were full of noises. I felt very quiet after my long day, and my baby began to be real to me. I don't mean that it hadn't been real before; but, now, in a way, I was alone with it. Sis had left the lights very low. She put on a Ray Charles record and sat on the sofa.<br/><br/>I lis</i></span>
  <span id="freeText5110243256204076679" style="display:none">When it comes to writing a review for this, I prefer to share a small afterword as I prefer to let Baldwin's words speak rather than mine.<br /><br /><i>"I sat on the hassock, leaning on Daddy's knee. Now, it was seven o'clock and the streets were full of noises. I felt very quiet after my long day, and my baby began to be real to me. I don't mean that it hadn't been real before; but, now, in a way, I was alone with it. Sis had left the lights very low. She put on a Ray Charles record and sat on the sofa.<br /><br />I listened to the music and the sounds from the streets and Daddy's hand rested lightly on my hair. And everything seemed connected--the street sounds, and Ray's voice and his piano and my Daddy's hand and my sister's silhouette and the sounds and the lights coming from the kitchen. It was as though we were a picture, trapped in time: this had been happening for hundreds of years, people sitting in a room, waiting for dinner, and listening to the blues. And it was as though, out of these elements, this patience, my Daddy's touch, the sounds of my mother in the kitchen, the way the light fell, the way the music continued beneath everything, the movement of Ernestine's head as she lit a cigarette, the movement of her hand as she dropped the match into the ashtray, the blurred human voices rising from the street, out of this rage and a steady, somehow triumphant sorrow, my baby was slowly being formed. I wondered if it would have Fonny's eyes. As someone had wondered, not, after all, so very long ago, about the eyes of Joseph, my father, whose hand rested on my head. What struck me suddenly, more than anything else, was something I knew but hadn't looked at: this was Fonny's baby and mine, we had made it together, it was both of us. I didn't know either of us very well. What would both of us be like? But this, somehow, made me think of Fonny and made me smile. My father rubbed his hand over my forehead. I thought of Fonny's touch, of Fonny, in my arms, his breath, his touch, his odor, his weight, that terrible and beautiful presence riding into me and his breath being snarled, as if by a golden thread, deeper and deeper in his throat as he rode--as he rode deeper and deeper not so much into me as into a kingdom which lay just behind his eyes. He worked on wood that way. He worked on stone that way. If I had never seen him work, I might never have known he loved me.<br /><br />It's a miracle to realize that somebody loves you."</i><br /><br />He was such an amazing writer. I have to space his works out, year by year, so I can enjoy what he has left to offer. After reading 'The Fire This Time', I was first brought into his world, the life he led, the life many people still lead. Secondly, I read 'Giovanni's Room' where I was introduced to his fictional prowess and couldn’t put the book down. Thirdly, was ‘Another Country’; I thought the first part of the book may have been some of the best writing I’ve ever read. Now, with ‘If Beale Street Could Talk’, I am satisfied to know that Baldwin is one of my favorite writers ever.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5110243256204076679" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2653403172" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2653403172" />
    <a title="Watch Your Words" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72369907-watch-your-words"><img alt="Watch Your Words" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544569098p2/72369907.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2653403172?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Watch Your Words" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Watch Your Words" href="/user/show/72369907-watch-your-words">Watch Your Words</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72369907-watch-your-words?shelf=books-from-the-podcast">books-from-the-podcast</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2653403172" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3673459635654528319"> At the center of Baldwin’s critique on the universality of oppression and pain is a love story that is both delicate and bold in its attempt to fight a system that would keep people of color from receiving the dignity they deserve. Through Tish, Baldwin has created not only a living breathing entity, that is so familiar you could mistake her for your own sister, but also a swirling metaphor for the power that earnest love can have on shaping the world around us and birthing the hope we all need</span>
  <span id="freeText3673459635654528319" style="display:none"> At the center of Baldwin’s critique on the universality of oppression and pain is a love story that is both delicate and bold in its attempt to fight a system that would keep people of color from receiving the dignity they deserve. Through Tish, Baldwin has created not only a living breathing entity, that is so familiar you could mistake her for your own sister, but also a swirling metaphor for the power that earnest love can have on shaping the world around us and birthing the hope we all need to continue our fight for freedom. In addition this novel serves as a frank commentary on how imperfect people can be seen perfectly through the eyes of someone who knows them so intimately that they are, as Baldwin puts it, “one body”. Tish expresses at one point that “it’s a miracle to realize that somebody loves you” and through the lives of all of these characters we get to see just exactly how much of a miracle love can be, especially when, against all odds, it is the only thing you have to hang on to.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3673459635654528319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2653403172">

<div id="review_1868852685" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1868852685" />
    <a title="Neal Adolph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5459072-neal-adolph"><img alt="Neal Adolph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1313534109p2/5459072.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1868852685?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Neal Adolph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Neal Adolph" href="/user/show/5459072-neal-adolph">Neal Adolph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5459072-neal-adolph?shelf=writers-of-colour">writers-of-colour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5459072-neal-adolph?shelf=2017-reads">2017-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5459072-neal-adolph?shelf=north-american">north-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5459072-neal-adolph?shelf=ebooks">ebooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1868852685" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11670048098013577795">I usually don't say that a book is unput-downable, because in my limited reading experience I have found very few books have been unput-downable. But here I am, late in the morning, waking up after unexpectedly finishing this book last night, having picked it up for a few more pages of progress before going to sleep, and then having been kept awake by the growing sense of unease and frustration until finally I finished it, turning that final electronic page, and feeling far from relieved by what</span>
  <span id="freeText11670048098013577795" style="display:none">I usually don't say that a book is unput-downable, because in my limited reading experience I have found very few books have been unput-downable. But here I am, late in the morning, waking up after unexpectedly finishing this book last night, having picked it up for a few more pages of progress before going to sleep, and then having been kept awake by the growing sense of unease and frustration until finally I finished it, turning that final electronic page, and feeling far from relieved by what I read, far from able to sleep, but exhausted because it was 4am.<br /><br />It is frustrating, I imagine, for people of colour in the most-free United States of America, to read this book, turn to its year of copyright, and discover that it is still, after more than 40 years, so goddamned relevant and so goddamned honest and ringing with so much goddamned truth. It was frustrating for me, a white Canadian, to watch the injustice in this story to develop, to know more and more the webs that made it possible, how deep they are, and how pointedly, carefully, and directly lies the blame in the heart of a single, red haired, blue-eyed, white-skinned human being with a dick, and badge, and a chip on his shoulder. <br /><br />This is a heart-breaking work.<br /><br />But, and I suspect this is true in ways that I don't yet know as a writer, it is most heart-breaking because it is most outrageously beautiful. The characters, their relationships, their love for each other and their hopes for the future. It is here, captured remarkably well, in language that would make even F. Scott Fitzgerald jealous for its sultry combination of sadness and sweet sweet romance. The microaggressions of love, layed out from one character to the next, love known and felt and acknowledged and reciprocated. Love being hurt by a loved one being hurt, altered, changed, broken, and still, amazingly, remaining beautifully strong.<br /><br />James Baldwin is damned good as a writer, but I don't think he wrote this book for me - he certainly had no reason to believe I would be reading it. No, this book is for the people about which it is written. And you can tell this by seeing his language, by seeing the ways in which he reminds these people that they are beautiful, remarkable, outrageous and bountiful humanity caught in, as they are well aware, a nation infested with the deeply burrowed maggots of racism. But this book is expertly written, and so I could read it, I could understand it just well enough, to not only recognize the horrifying dissonance of the black life in America - the one in which you are treated as an incomplete form of humanity while you and the small world of other black bodies that you are in know the contrary is entirely true - but to, beyond recognition, actually get a sense, for an instant or two, for less than 200 hundred pages, just what that dissonance can feel like, just how deeply it can cut, just how closely you can feel the freedoms and rights which don't exist, just how much hope there must be in a future of liberty. <br /><br />This book is a reminder of the dignity found in those who have been and are being oppressed, those who are looking for ways to survive despite all the forces working against them, and of the dignity bound up in hope and in family and in love. <br /><br />Just in case you didn't know it, just in case you've ever doubted it, just in case you have ever wondered what it could possibly mean, read this book, because #BlackLivesMatter, and this book has made that ringing truth even more real for me. Thank you Mr. James Baldwin. I'll be back to read more of you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11670048098013577795" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2583762102" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2583762102" />
    <a title="Jessaka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4659464-jessaka"><img alt="Jessaka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485709659p2/4659464.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2583762102?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessaka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessaka" href="/user/show/4659464-jessaka">Jessaka</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19806917-if-beale-street-could-talk">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4659464-jessaka?shelf=african-american">african-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2583762102" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8898116175893466178">Bluesy it Ain’t. “Twas More Like Rap Gone Bad<br/><br/>I wanted to learn about Beale Street, and the blues, and the lives of the artists that lived there, but this book was about a man and a woman who had fallen in love.<br/><br/>When reading the first three pages <br/>I saw the corner of a dark room,<br/>a home of a black friend’s friend<br/>or maybe I knew her too, but<br/>I don’t remember who she was,<br/>only that we went to the same church.<br/><br/>Across the street from her house<br/>there was a white picket fence, and<br/>I only knew that I was in Ric</span>
  <span id="freeText8898116175893466178" style="display:none">Bluesy it Ain’t. “Twas More Like Rap Gone Bad<br /><br />I wanted to learn about Beale Street, and the blues, and the lives of the artists that lived there, but this book was about a man and a woman who had fallen in love.<br /><br />When reading the first three pages <br />I saw the corner of a dark room,<br />a home of a black friend’s friend<br />or maybe I knew her too, but<br />I don’t remember who she was,<br />only that we went to the same church.<br /><br />Across the street from her house<br />there was a white picket fence, and<br />I only knew that I was in Richmond.<br /><br />And then the image was gone,<br />and I could not get it back. <br />Was it really real,<br />or did my mind make this all up,<br />and if it did, why?<br /><br />And the book began blasé like that,<br />like an old record that had lost its beat.<br /><br />But then Baldwin found his Voice<br />when Tish and Fonny made<br />beautiful love,<br />which must have been his own aphrodisiac,<br /> because his writing came Alive.<br /><br />But the sex and the vulgarity<br />were all too much for me,<br />well, maybe it wasn’t the sex<br />but the vulgarity in every definition<br />of the word.<br /><br />I wanted to put the book away, and I <br />believed that the injustices in their world <br />would ruin their lives forever anyway.<br /><br />Tish was the woman who loved that man, Foony, who then had a baby by him, a man who was now in prison waiting for his trial. And they really couldn’t afford a lawyer, nor could they afford to live in the Housing Projects, which were horrible anyway, and reminded me of what I had read about tenant housing in New York when immigrants first came to America. Rats, cockroaches. I have known people who lived in houses like this, and whether they rented or owned, that was just how they lived. <br /><br />I was invited for breakfast one morning by a woman who lived across the street from us in Fresno. I walked into her kitchen and sat down. Under my feet was an indoor/outdoor carpet filled with old dried food, maybe even some fresh; it didn’t matter which, because the smell was enough to make you gag. The filth was everywhere. And on the walls, the counters, and the stove, cockroaches were doing a fearless slow waltz through her kitchen. I sat there eating my breakfast watching it all, and when I finished eating, I returned no more. And she never even realized that her living conditions were deplorable. This book was like that, and you have to have the stomach for it too. Only Tish and Fonny knew that by living in America their lives were deplorable; they knew that much.<br /><br />Note: Beale Street isn’t in Harlem where the story is set. It is in Memphis, Tennessee, but maybe by bringing the two together Baldwin’s was being artsy or was saying that all black streets were the same.<br /><br />Tidbit: If Beale Street could talk it would tell you go have lunch at the King’s Palace, because they have the best breaded fried green tomatoes and crab cakes. Then you may wish to go find some blues music, maybe at B.B. King’s. I hear that they serve good food too.</span>

<div id="review_2035186696" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2035186696" />
    <a title="Bobbieshiann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39394999-bobbieshiann"><img alt="Bobbieshiann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496365582p2/39394999.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2035186696?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bobbieshiann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bobbieshiann" href="/user/show/39394999-bobbieshiann">Bobbieshiann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39394999-bobbieshiann?shelf=one-time-was-not-enough">one-time-was-not-enough</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39394999-bobbieshiann?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2035186696" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8934351886106939000">81 pages in I cried real tears of understanding while curled up in my bed. I don’t think I’ve ever read so much passion like this before. If Beale Street Could Talk brought forth so many lessons for me. I kept thinking that this Black love must be sacred. It cannot be broken because the power within it creates a foundation that sets a tone of purity and honesty. Tish and Fonny are a young couple who’s love has no limits. They both come from poverty but both families lead in two different directi</span>
  <span id="freeText8934351886106939000" style="display:none">81 pages in I cried real tears of understanding while curled up in my bed. I don’t think I’ve ever read so much passion like this before. If Beale Street Could Talk brought forth so many lessons for me. I kept thinking that this Black love must be sacred. It cannot be broken because the power within it creates a foundation that sets a tone of purity and honesty. Tish and Fonny are a young couple who’s love has no limits. They both come from poverty but both families lead in two different directions. <br /><br />Through the peak of their love, Fonny is arrested and Tish is pregnant. With the odds against them, they have a bond that no barrier can truly separate. They understand that they must fight until Fonny is free and that is just what they did! <br /><br />If Beale Street Could Talk clearly showed the division between Black people. How we have played right into the hands of the man and now, we do the work for the man. We belittle each other, venture away from each other, but we also prove others wrong. Tish’s family is built on love alone and they will do anything to protect each other. <br /><br />“The poor are always crossing the Sahara. And lawyers and bondsmen and all that crowd circle around the poor, exactly like vultures”. James is unapologetic as he tells the story of what it is like to be a Black man in America. Through all this he still found a way to write about love. To write about survival. To showcase beauty! <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8934351886106939000" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3312340210" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11165741588610374877">Published in 1974 and set in Harlem, this book is part love story and part condemnation of racial injustice. Tish and Fonny have known each other since they were children. Tish narrates the story, telling how they became a couple and how Fonny ended up in prison for a crime he did not commit. Tish, her family, and Fonny’s father try their best to prove his innocence. This couple maintains a sense of optimism in the face of extreme hardship through their love for each other. It is a sad and distu</span>
  <span id="freeText11165741588610374877" style="display:none">Published in 1974 and set in Harlem, this book is part love story and part condemnation of racial injustice. Tish and Fonny have known each other since they were children. Tish narrates the story, telling how they became a couple and how Fonny ended up in prison for a crime he did not commit. Tish, her family, and Fonny’s father try their best to prove his innocence. This couple maintains a sense of optimism in the face of extreme hardship through their love for each other. It is a sad and disturbing story of racism and corruption in the criminal justice system. Unfortunately, these topics remain relevant in today’s society. This is the fifth book I have ready by James Baldwin and his writing is consistently strong. </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('viEiz1PzXwj7fQvS9UOZsbyaZ8BnNW1XEeyv3mhpR8OIWk2gjr4jw8mzEIj1hkjZET7oWPnQsBYD0S8bXvlAdQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('iwcAlRnNEfKJEEvXAyZarLlRTiyZp1d0uo14xK+3Jiy9fG/6xIBtObveUI0D44vEFPXBtAdCijWosPgBmSchmg==')})
  }

Elwood’s only salvation is his friendship with fellow “delinquent” Turner, which deepens despite Turner’s conviction that Elwood is hopelessly naive, that the world is crooked, and that the only way to survive is to scheme and avoid trouble. As life at the Academy becomes ever more perilous, the tension between Elwood’s ideals and Turner’s skepticism leads to a decision whose repercussions will echo down the decades.\n \nBased on the real story of a reform school that operated for 111 years and warped the lives of thousands of children, The Nickel Boys is a devastating, driven narrative that showcases a great American novelist writing Americans have built an empire on the idea of “race,” a falsehood that damages us all but falls most heavily on the bodies of black women and men—bodies exploited through slavery and segregation, and, today, threatened, locked up, and murdered out of all proportion. What is it like to inhabit a black body and find a way to live within it? And how can we all honestly reckon with this fraught history and free ourselves from its burden?  Between the World and Me is Ta-Nehisi Coates’s attempt to answer these questions in a letter to his adolescent son. Coates shares with his son—and readers—the story of his awakening to the truth about his place in the world through a series of revelatory experiences, from Howard University to Civil War battlefields, from the South Side of Chicago to Paris, from his childhood home to the living rooms of mothers whose children’s lives were taken as American plunder. Beautifully woven from personal narrative, reimagined history, and fresh, emotionally charged reportage, Between the World and Me clearly illuminates the past, bracingly confronts our present, and offers a transcendent vision for a way forward.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"4760859930801604143\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n Alexander shows that, by targeting black men and decimating communities of color, the U.S. criminal justice system functions as a contemporary system of racial control, even as it formally adheres to the principle of color blindness. <a href="/author/show/10427.James_Baldwin">James Baldwin

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4573341110286617039"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/44217-master-list-of-author-disambiguation-by-spaces' rel='nofollow noopener'>See this thread for more information.</a></i><br/><br/>James Arthur Baldwin was an American novelist, essayist, playwright, poet, and social critic.<br/><br/>James Baldwin offered a vital literary voice during the era of civil rights activism in the 1950s and '60s. He was the eldest of nine children; his stepfather was a minister. At age </span>
  <span id="freeText4573341110286617039" style="display:none"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/44217-master-list-of-author-disambiguation-by-spaces" rel="nofollow noopener">See this thread for more information.</a></i><br /><br />James Arthur Baldwin was an American novelist, essayist, playwright, poet, and social critic.<br /><br />James Baldwin offered a vital literary voice during the era of civil rights activism in the 1950s and '60s. He was the eldest of nine children; his stepfather was a minister. At age 14, Baldwin became a preacher at the small Fireside Pentecostal Church in Harlem. In the early 1940s, he transferred his faith from religion to literature. Critics, however, note the impassioned cadences of Black churches are still evident in his writing. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17143.Go_Tell_It_on_the_Mountain" title="Go Tell It on the Mountain" rel="nofollow noopener">Go Tell It on the Mountain</a>, his first novel, is a partially autobiographical account of his youth. His essay collections <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/410810.Notes_of_a_Native_Son" title="Notes of a Native Son" rel="nofollow noopener">Notes of a Native Son</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38458.Nobody_Knows_My_Name" title="Nobody Knows My Name" rel="nofollow noopener">Nobody Knows My Name</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/464260.The_Fire_Next_Time" title="The Fire Next Time" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fire Next Time</a> were influential in informing a large white audience.<br /><br />From 1948, Baldwin made his home primarily in the south of France, but often returned to the USA to lecture or teach. In 1957, he began spending half of each year in New York City. His novels include <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38462.Giovanni_s_Room" title="Giovanni's Room" rel="nofollow noopener">Giovanni's Room</a>, about a white American expatriate who must come to terms with his homosexuality, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38474.Another_Country" title="Another Country" rel="nofollow noopener">Another Country</a>, about racial and gay sexual tensions among New York intellectuals. His inclusion of gay themes resulted in a lot of savage criticism from the Black community. Eldridge Cleaver, of the Black Panthers, stated the Baldwin's writing displayed an "agonizing, total hatred of blacks." Baldwin's play, Blues for Mister Charlie, was produced in 1964. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38469.Going_to_Meet_the_Man" title="Going to Meet the Man" rel="nofollow noopener">Going to Meet the Man</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/38461.Tell_Me_How_Long_the_Train_s_Been_Gone" title="Tell Me How Long the Train's Been Gone" rel="nofollow noopener">Tell Me How Long the Train's Been Gone</a> provided powerful descriptions of American racism. As an openly gay man, he became increasingly outspoken in condemning discrimination against lesbian and gay people.<br /><br />On November 30, 1987 Baldwin died from stomach cancer in Saint-Paul-de-Vence, France. He was buried at the Ferncliff Cemetery in Hartsdale, near New York City.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4573341110286617039" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
